  151. I bought the no!no! because I thought it would be a good alternative to laser or waxing. I thought it was a handheld laser-type device. I was wrong. (I have since returned it.) What the no!no! is, is a handheld hair burning (or singeing) device. It uses a heated/electrified wire to burn the hair off, above the skin. Nothing happens below the skin to the hair root. (If the hair root is unaffected, you will still have hair growth.) Enter the buffing pad (essentially this is extremely fine sandpaper). The instructions say that you MUST use the buffing pad every time you use the no!no!. The reason for this is because the buffing pad is actually what removes the hair, exfoliates your skin and discourages hair growth in the future. Example: consider an overweight person who constantly wears tight jeans that rub on certain parts of the legs. The hair will not continue to grow on the back of the calves, for instance, because the jeans rub everyday on that part of the skin and the constant rubbing/buffing removes the hair and discourages future hair growth.<br /><br />You can buy hair removal products that are essentially sand paper for just a few dollars at a number of retailers. In my opinion, the makers of no!no! are simply price-gouging customers (and wasting their time by burning the hair first) in order to sell buffing pads. Save your money and your time, don't buy the no!no!

  256. I admit, I have thick black hair on my legs. This looks particularly bad since I also happen to have pale skin. Based on its advertising, and recommendations from the folks at Sephora, I thought No! No! would be a great solution.<br /><br />Doesn't work at all. The way the product is designed, there's basically a sort of thin electric wire that just burns off the top of the hair. The hair grows right back. The infomercials and various sellers advertise it as a product that uses electricity to crystalize the hair or some such. Well, it doesn't get hot enough to get all the way down to the follicle. Not even close. It doesn't even burn off the entire top of the hair. You have to \\"buff\\" the lower bit off. So, actually, it's worse than shaving.<br /><br />I've since had laser hair removal, so I now know how hot something has to get for a follicle to die. I also know what a \\"killed\\" follicle looks like and let me tell you, the NoNo has never even came close.<br /><br />However, I'd seen good reviews on this product too though, so I actually tried it for a few months. The result? No reduction in hair growth, no real thinning of the hair. The hair looks thinner to a certain extent because you're not getting shaving stubble, but you'd get the same result from waxing. Or, better, since you wouldn't have to deal with the horrible smell of burning hair.<br /><br />For those people who say they've seen \\"reduction.\\" Your probably the ones using the NoNo ALL THE TIME. You're causing your hair growing cycles to stagger, like when I get patchy hair growth after frequent waxing/shaving. Don't believe me? Try it. It should turn out fine if the NoNo does what it says it does and has permanently removed your hair, right?<br /><br />Finally, let me just break down all the \\"science\\" behind the NoNo. Thermicon = heat. Thermal energy transfer = It gets hot. Thermal wire = Hot wire. Crystalize = It burns up, and turns crispy. Based on this, NoNo advertises itself as\\"technically and scientifically sound.\\" Notice, they don't say it's scientifically proven, BECAUSE THEY CAN'T. They only say the theory on which the technology operates is scientifically POSSIBLE. And, yes, it is possible to heat up a hair follicle and kill it. It's even theoretically possible to use hair to conduct enough heat down to the follicle causing it to die. Actually possible? Not so much. Hair is a very poor heat conductor, that's why it keeps your head warm/cool. That's why all your hair doesn't fall out every time you use a curling iron.
